APPENDIX III

ROTARY INTERNATIONAL AND DISTRICT 5730:
FINANCIAL ASPECTS
 EFFECTIVE JUNE 30, 1997

 CLUBS Rotarians paid dues to their club for the support and maintenance of their club. The club’s Board of Directors determined the amount of dues to be paid and included an amount to cover the membership of that club in District 5730 and Rotary International. In District 5730 club dues ranged from quite modest to quite substantial, dependent mostly on projects that a club might support through its dues structure.

District 5730 Voting delegates representing each club in the district determined the member assessment paid by clubs to the District Treasury. In 1996-97 the amount was $14 per club member per year. That figure was based on a proposed district budget submitted by the Incoming District Governor at the annual District Conference. Invariably the actual money spent was less than that proposed because Governors tended to be tight fisted with district funds, coupled with the fact that incoming governors were often submitting blind estimates. The District Advisory Council took corrective action concerning this problem at its May, 1997 meeting in Lubbock while continuing to insist on frugality.

There was an active array of projects supported by the District with funding; however, most expenses were paid from other sources. Three district projects, Boys’ RYLA, Girls’ RYLA, and the Diabetic Camp, were supported by tuition paid by clubs and based on the number of participants each club sponsored. Costs for the District Youth Exchange program were paid by parents, host families, and clubs. Ambassadorial Scholarships were funded by Rotary International. Group Study Exchange programs were funded by Rotary International with hosting families provided housing, some meals and local transportation. World Community Service projects were supported by clubs while the District Apple Orchard was funded by an initial grant of $10,000 and thereafter by voluntary contributions. Friendship Exchange was supported by those who participated in it and their hosting families.

The Office of the Governor Money was invested in the training of governors. This proved to be a wise investment in the district leadership and made significant contributions to the welfare of the district. The incoming governor and spouse attended the Rotary International Assembly at the expense of Rotary International. Their travel and hotel expenses were paid by the district to attended the International Convention and Zone Institute. After these three meetings, expenses incurred by spouses were the responsibility of the DGN/DG/PDG. The district assisted with expenses incurred by the DGN/DG while attending PETS.

The Governor received a budget from Rotary International based upon the number of clubs in the district and the mileage required for travel. It helped with postage, phone, printing, gasoline, and minimal office services but every governor reached in his pocket for many subsidies. There were many meetings, both in the district and outside of it, that the governor was called to attend for which there was no reimbursement.

The Representative of the President at the District Conference Rotarians of distinction were appointed by the President of Rotary International as his representative at the annual District Conference. The expenses of travel for the representative and spouse were borne by Rotary International. The District Conference provided registration, room, meals, local transportation and a gift.

District Representative to the Council on Legislation Once every three years a delegate is elected at the District Conference to represent the district at the triennial council. Rotary International paid for travel, meals, and hotel for the delegate. Attending spouse’s expenses were borne by the delegate.

In 1997 Rotary clubs were billed by Rotary International @ $1.00 per club member to cover the costs of the forthcoming legislative council. The next assessment will not occur for three years and the amount will be determined by vote at the 1997-98 Council On Legislation.

The Office of Director of Rotary International Directors of Rotary International were elected for two year terms and represented different Zones of R.I. Directors received no funds from clubs or districts within the Zone which elected them.

As a general officer of Rotary International, the Director received an allowance for the conduct of their office. This partially covered the expenses of phone, fax, postage, printing, and minimal secretarial services. The Director’s official travel expenses for Rotary International business was reimbursed. The numerous invitations that an incoming Director, Director, or Past Director of R.I. received from clubs and districts to attend special functions were accepted as a personal expense unless the inviting group offered to help with expenses, unfortunately a rare happening. (Ed. Note: two observations: they don’t turn down invitations and they don’t ask for expenses.)

 During the two year term of office Rotary International pays the expenses of the Director and spouse to attend the following functions: the International Assembly, International Convention, the Council on Legislation, all Board of Director’s meetings, representing the President of R.I. at District Conferences, and some committee meetings of the Board. After the term of office ends they pursue Rotary at their own expense with only a few exceptions noted above and below.

Committees and Task Forces of Rotary International These groups are made up of Presidential appointments from the cadre of past district governors, past directors, and past presidents of R.I. At the time these committees and task forces are approved by the Board of R.I. a budget for the activity is approved. Some of these groups conduct their activities by mail at the member’s expense. Others meet from time to time and members are compensated for their expenses by R.I.

The President of Rotary International A nominating committee composed of past directors of R.I. submitted the name of a Rotarian for the office of President and that person was elected at the International Convention. A budget was established by the Board of Directors for his travel and miscellaneous expenses. No salary was involved. Rotary International owned a condominium near the World Headquarters in Evanston. This was for the sole use of the President as his residence during his term of office and offered a modicum of comfort and a place for small social events.

Conclusion Elected positions in Rotary had their expenses compensated to a reasonable degree. Frequently things arose that needed to be done that were not covered in a budget. Dedication to Rotary and “Service Above Self” caused many officers to do them anyway. These two concepts were one of the great successes of Rotary, not for just elected officials, but for Rotarians in general: men and women who gave so much of themselves for this great organization. Satisfaction of efforts put forth came in many ways, the least of which was money.

APPENDIX IV

BOYS’ RYLA
A PROJECT OF MAJOR PROPORTIONS
WRITTEN BY THOSE WHO DID IT

Begun in 1976 by District Governor John Tyler (Midland,) under the leadership of Tom Amason (Amarillo West,) District 5730’s Boys’ RYLA (Rotary Youth Leadership Award) presented an annual leadership experience to an estimated 1800 high school seniors in the Texas Panhandle through the 1996-97 Rotary year.

Outstanding speakers from many walks of life devoted some of their time to share their experiences and perceptions with these boys, each of whom was a recognized leader in their respective high schools. Professional people from across the United States came, often at their own expense, to present their programs. One such speaker journeyed from Scotland. The list included personalities who were at the top of their field such as physicians, military leaders, attorneys, bankers, chief executive officers, and diplomats.

Dedicated Rotarians from across the District repeatedly have offered their services. Every Past District Governor, beginning with PDG John Tyler, has given varying amounts of support to this non-district funded undertaking. Every club in the District has sent boys to RYLA. Some clubs sponsored boys intermittently but the majority of them sponsored students on a regular basis.

Since its beginning more than $500,000 was budgeted for the RYLA camps. The number of campers averaged about 75 boys each year while the number of Rotary volunteers approached 30. It is estimated that more than 3,000 ‘man days’ have been devoted by these Rotarians to the young men of District 5730 since 1976.

Through 1996 RYLA was held at Camp Summer Life which was located south of Taos, New Mexico, giving boys ‘from the flat lands’ a chance to experience the 8,000 high, cool, mountains of Northern New Mexico. In 1997 the camp location was changed to The Lone Tree Ranch in Capitan near Ruidoso, New Mexico.

Other Boys’ RYLA camps were formed as a direct result of attending 5730’s annual activity. Districts in Texas, other neighboring states, Canada, and Scotland initiated RYLA camps after observing 5730’s successes.

Campers from past years were invited back to share their experiences and thoughts about RYLA. Without exception each ‘ex’ camper was highly supportive of RYLA. The 5 days of each camp was the formation of many lifelong friendships.

District 5730’s Boys’ RYLA Camp was a tribute to all district Rotarians, past and present, who were involved and there were many: those in each club who selected their club’s representatives, those who served as Counselors, and those who planned and directed each year’s undertaking. To try to name all of them would be difficult as well as hazardous. Many Rotarians in the District were responsible for making RYLA happen. Those efforts were rewarded and made worthwhile by the young men who emerged from each RYLA camp.

(Ed. Note: Walter Johnson (Amarillo,) a 21 year veteran of Boys’ RYLA, and others provided this information.)

APPENDIX V

GIRLS RYLA
A SUMMARY BY THOSE WHO MADE IT WORK

The first Girls’ RYLA was held in 1985 at Camp Summerlife in the mountains near Taos, New Mexico.

Billye (Mrs. Ralph) McLaughlin, Big Spring, volunteered to lay the groundwork for a girls’ RYLA, provided the Rotarians would name a Director for the camp. Don Markham (Amarillo East) was named Camp Director for the first year, 1985.

Lea (Mrs. Bob) Covington (Odessa) was the first volunteer and she and Billye McLaughlin worked together for several years. Billye served as Program chair and Lea was the Registrar. One of the tedious jobs that befell the registrar was to ensure that each girl was assigned to a cabin where she knew no one else, not even the counselor. This was a difficult and time consuming task but paid handsome dividends in getting the girls to learn how to make friends. The acid test came one year when twins who had never been separated were assigned different cabins. The results were phenomenal.

1985-1986 Camp Summer Life near Taos, New Mexico was the site of the second Girls’ RYLA camp. Rex Wilt (Amarillo East) became Camp Director with his wife Michelle as his assistant. Billye and Lea retained their same titles. Each year at the end of camp the girls were given a critique sheet. They were asked to rate the speakers and other programs on a scale of 1 - 10. Those receiving the highest ratings were invited to return the following year. One of the more significant observations was that the girls didn’t like the type of programs that the boys were receiving - their needs were different. In starting Girls’ RYLA, the programs were patterned pretty much the same as for Boys’ RYLA. As a result of these critiques it was decided that fewer speakers and more workshop programs would better serve the girls.

1986 - 1987 Rex Wilt was Camp Director. Billye McLaughlin served as Program Chair with Lea Covington as Assistant Program Chair, and Bob Covington became Registrar. For the 1987 camp held at Camp Summer Life near Taos, New Mexico Lea designed several “workshops” for the girls. They were designed to let the girls work out and solve problems among themselves. There were “image building,” leadership, and goal oriented workshops. Although the girls were recognized as leaders they really had no idea why or how they had achieved this status. They were leaders but did not fully understand how to handle their leadership roles. The workshops helped them to focus on these problems and were deemed to be a huge success.

1987 - 1988 The officers of the previous year agreed to serve once again. This camp was held at Ceta Glen near Canyon, Texas.

1988 - 1989 Billye and PDG Ralph McLaughlin became Camp Co-Directors. Kae McLaughlin assumed the role of Program Director and Howard Wilkerson (Canyon) was Registrar. This camp, as well as those following were held Camp Summer Life near Taos, New Mexico.

1989 - 1990 Billye McLaughlin was Camp Director. Other officers remained the same for this year.

1990 - 1993 Larry Johnson (Odessa) assumed the District Chair of Girls RYLA. Dr. Cheri Sparks (Big Spring) was Camp Director, and Linda Conway (Big Spring) functioned as Registrar.

1993 - 1995 Dr. Cheri Sparks became the District Chair of Girls’ RYLA. Linda Conway was the Camp Director and Sheryl Shults (Big Spring) filled the position of Registrar/Treasurer.

1995 - 1997 Linda Conway was named as District Chair of Girls’ RYLA. Claudia Heathington (O’Donnell) became the Camp Director/Program Chair and Sheryl Shults remained as Registrar/Treasurer.

There were many counselors and volunteers during the years but the one who gave the most was Susan (Mrs. Bob) Lewis (Big Spring.) She missed one year and was a speaker every year she was there, a counselor several years, plus helping with the workshops and anything else that was needed. Her award winning speech, “Liberty,” described in Chapter VII was very popular with the girls.

Charlotte (Mrs. John) Seay (Midland) was another tremendous worker in Girls’ RYLA. She is a well known artist and came to share her talents with the girls. Art to many of them was something they may have considered but had never been involved in. Charlotte offered her talents to the girls during the afternoon “freetime” and it became one of the more popular activities.

In recognition of the work performed by Lea Covington for Girls’ RYLA, a Paul Harris award was presented to her by Charleen Formby (Midland.)

Rotarians throughout the district followed the leadership of the Big Spring and Odessa Rotarians and spouses who caused Girls’ RYLA to happen. Few clubs that sponsored a girl for the camp failed to continue in the program for the rewards were evident. The enrollment was constant at about 80 girls.

Girls’ RYLA volunteers reap their rewards from the many success stories that continued to recur because of their efforts. They have seen girls whose lives have been dramatically enhanced by their experiences at RYLA. Who would not be excited when given an opportunity to make a difference in the lives of tomorrow’s leaders?

(Editor’s note: Billye McLaughlin (Big Spring) provided this information with assistance from others involved in this project.)

APPENDIX VI

TECHNICAL TRAINING AWARDS
AMBASSADORIAL SCHOLARSHIPS

Technical Training Awards, a program of the Rotary Foundation, was of short duration in the early 1970s. The program provided a grant to promising young people who wished to further their technical training in post secondary schools abroad. In 1970-71 two in-bound students were hosted in the District: one from Australia who was counseled by R. Lewis Smith of the Amarillo North East club and one from Germany who was counseled by Murry Kovner of the Lubbock club. In 1973-74 one additional student from the Philippines was counseled by an unidentified Amarillo Rotarian.

Ambassadorial Scholarships was one of the Rotary Foundation’s longest running and best known programs. The educational awards program was begun in 1947 and District 5730 was a participant in it for many of the ensuing years. Contributions to the Rotary Foundation determined the number of scholarships offered and in later years was based on the Foundation’s SHARE guidelines. The District Educational Awards Committee determined the type of scholarships to be offered and made the selection from qualified candidates who applied for them. Criteria used by the Committee insured a high quality of applicants who were potential ambassadors of goodwill.

Following is an incomplete, and probably inaccurate record of scholarship recipients since 1970.

APPENDIX VII

YOUTH EXCHANGE

 Friendship, understanding and peace among nations were the goals of this program and those ends were pursued by enabling students of high school age (15 - 18) to spend time abroad living and studying while staying with and being supervised by hosting families. This program had its beginnings in the Community Service Avenue and remained there until 1984-85 when it became a part of the International Service jurisdiction.

District 5730 did not participate in Youth Exchange activities to any large extent until DeWitt Thompson (Lubbock) assumed the chair of this committee in 1980-81. What started as a trickle of students that year became a torrent of young people visiting abroad while communities hosted their counterparts throughout the district. During the past 17 years literally hundreds of inbound and outbound students have traveled abroad and in almost all cases the experiences have proved fruitful. Students were given opportunities to spend a summer in the program or a full academic year, being hosted by three different families. The program quickly grew from being administered out of DeWitt’s office to a highly organized district-wide committee involving many Rotarians. The organization for 1997-98 was headed by the general chair who was assisted by chairmen for Inbound Students, Outbound Students, Outbound Recruitment & Communications, Interviews, Insurance and Splash Down activities. The district chairs providing the tremendous effort required by this program were:

1980 - 1985 DeWitt Thompson R. C. of Lubbock
1985 - 1990 Richard Damron R. C. of Amarillo
1991 - 1996 Lee George Big Spring Rotary Club
1996 - 1997 Bob Strange Gtr. Lubbock R. C.


APPENDIX VIII

GROUP STUDY EXCHANGE

Group Study Exchange was a significant program of the Rotary Foundation. Grants enabled outstanding non-Rotarian business and professional persons to travel between paired Rotary districts in different countries. Teams spent four to six weeks abroad where they studied their host country’s institutions, economy and lifestyles; observed their own professions in that country; and developed friendships and discussed ideas with local citizens. Teams were composed of varying numbers. Our first GSE team had six members plus a Rotarian team leader. Later ones consisted of four members plus a Rotarian team leader. Following is a listing of GSE programs conducted by District 5730.

YEAR PAIRED DISTRICT TEAM LEADER

1973-74 Dist. 141, Finland H. Oberhelman, Lubbock

1985-87 Dist. 198, Switzerland Pat Northcutt, Dalhart

 APPENDIX IX

THE DIABETIC CAMP
A PROJECT OF THE SOUTHWEST LUBBOCK ROTARY CLUB
AND DISTRICT 5730

 In 1977 an idea was discussed among members of the Southwest Lubbock Rotary Club regarding children with diabetes. A need was identified by club members and discussion soon produced action to address the problem. Resources were identified, plans were made, and in 1978 the first summer camp for children with diabetes was organized. Information about the camp was dispensed through the Texas Tech School of Health Science Center’s Department of Pediatrics to clinics throughout the region served by the school. The reaction by the medical community and parents was predictable.

The six day camp’s objectives were to bring children between the ages of 6 - 15 with diabetes together, have fun, teach them about the physical, emotional, and dietetic aspect of the disease and address the problems of self esteem that might be present.

Dr. Surendra Varma and Dr. Michael Bourgeois provided the medical expertise for the camp and were assisted by many others from the medical community including nurses from Lubbock’s general hospitals and Texas Tech, medical school students, social workers, and a clinical psychologist and graduate students. About 30 professionals comprised the resources for the camp.

A typical day at the camp started at 7:00 AM. Campers tested their blood sugar, injected their first insulin shot, ate breakfast, participated in activities, ate a mid-morning snack, tested their blood sugar before lunch, ate lunch, rested, participated in more activities, met in group sessions where the management of diabetes was discussed, tested their blood sugar, injected their second insulin shot, ate supper, and participated in evening activities. Meal times were teaching times, stressing consistency of diet.

The Southwest Lubbock Rotary Club sponsored the camp until 1984 when it became a District 5730 project. Rotary Clubs throughout the District began paying for the costs of sponsoring about 60 children with diabetes who attended the camp each year.

The camp was originally held at Camp Butman near Merkle, Texas each summer. It was later moved to Ceta Glen Christian Camp near Canyon, Texas.

APPENDIX X

ROTARY DISTRICT 5730 FRIENDSHIP EXCHANGE

Rotary Friendship Exchange was established in District 5730 in 1992 under the leadership of PDG Charles Beil (Big Spring.) It was a marvelous opportunity for Rotarians, who may be accompanied by spouses, to improve international understanding and make new Rotary friends around the world. The concept was simply that Rotarians of one district host those of a foreign district for two weeks and then be hosted by the foreign district for a comparable period at a later time. Travelers were responsible for personal transportation expenses and hosts were responsible for housing, meals and local entertainment. District 5730 policy required a $100 fee from each traveler to defray expenses of establishing exchanges and entertaining visitors.

Members of the district committee who assisted PDG Beil with the first exchange included Gertrude Thompson (Amarillo East,) PDG Paige Carruth (Amarillo,) PDG Bob Lewis (Big Spring,) and PDG Ed Self (Plainview.) In 1996-97 the Committee consisted of Chairman Beil, PDG Carruth, Richard Griffith (Lubbock,) John Willis (Odessa,) Jack Miller (Plainview,) and James Robinson (Plainview.)

By the end of the 1996-97 Rotary year, exchanges were established with England, New Zealand, Chile, Sweden, South Africa and Western Australia. The status of each of these exchanges were as follows:

1. England, D1070, was completed. Seventeen guests visited District 5730 in September, 1994. The group was led by Tom Glover and was hosted in Big Spring, Plainview and Amarillo. Twenty travelers from District 5730 visited England in June, 1995. The group was led by PDG Charles Beil (Big Spring.)
2. New Zealand, D9940 was completed. Twenty-three guests visited District 5730 in October, 1995. The group was hosted in Amarillo, Plainview, Big Spring and Midland. Eighteen travelers from District 5730 visited New Zealand in March, 1996. They were led by Tom Beck (Amarillo.)
3. Chile, D4340 was completed. Four Rotarians visited District 5730 in April, 1996. They were led by Elena T. Sequel and were hosted in Big Spring, Lubbock, and Perryton. Six Rotarians from District 5730 visited Chile in September, 1997 and Chairman Beil was the team leader.
4. Sweden, D2390 was one-half completed. Ten Rotarians from clubs in Amarillo, Canyon and Big Spring visited Sweden in June, 1997. The group was led by PDG Beil. Sixteen travelers from Sweden were expected to visit District 5730 in September, 1998.
5. South Africa, D9270 was one-half completed. Nine Rotarians from clubs in Big Spring, Midland, Odessa, and Plainview visited South Africa in June, 1997. The team leader was Lynn Simpson (Big Spring.) No date was established for the South African’s return visit.
6. Western Australia, D9460 was in the planning stages. Sixteen Australian travelers were invited to come to District 5730 in April, 1998. No date was established for District 5730’s return visit but it was anticipated that a coordinated visit to Perth and the Rotary International Convention scheduled for Singapore in June, 1999 would occur. This exchange was being publicized at the time of this writing.

The Friendship Exchange program was a relatively new program in District 5730 and across Rotary*. The program was being adopted by more districts and offering more opportunities for Rotarians to become involved. Chairman Beil felt that the District Committee needed to be expanded, more programs given about the advantages of Friendship Exchange, more clubs involved as hosts, and more Rotarians participating in the projects. He felt that this great project of Rotary, Friendship Exchange, was one of the best ways to foster international understanding, the ultimate objective of the International Avenue of Service and he was working hard at promoting it.

(*Almost 80 Rotarians and spouses were guests of a club in England and paid a visit to London in 1978. In 1982 a large group from England was hosted in District 573 prior to the International Convention. Several clubs in the District have conducted visits with clubs in Mexico for many years, not formally organized through the Friendship Exchange program, but because it was fun and the right thing to do.)

APPENDIX XI

 ROTARY DISTRICT 5730 APPLE ORCHARD
A PROJECT TO FEED THE HUNGARY

“To be young is to sit under trees you did not plant.
To be a Rotarian is to plant trees under which you will never sit.”

In the early 1990’s Dr. George Elle, retired assistant dean of Agriculture at Texas Tech University and member of the Rotary Club of Lubbock, made an offer to the South Plains Food Bank that could not be refused. He offered to plant an apple orchard on the 29 acres of land donated to the Food Bank by Bill and Mary Louise Kingsbery. The land had originally been given to the Food Bank as a site for the Breedlove Dehydration Plant. Dr. Elle said that he would devote the next four years of his life on this great project if funds could be obtained to buy trees and equipment to start an orchard.

PDG Cliff Breaud (Lubbock) and PDG James Cook (Amarillo West) thought that an apple orchard would be a great force in uniting the members of District 5730 in a project that would help reduce the number of hungry people in the District. The idea of an orchard as a District project was presented to Governor Don Williams’ (Odessa) District Advisory Board in 1994. It was an idea the group felt Rotarians throughout the District would readily support. The Advisory Board recommended that Rotary District 5730 lend $10,000 to the Apple Orchard Committee to get the project started. A year later at the District Conference the debt was forgiven.

In April, 1994 Rotarians Elle, Mercer, and food bank volunteers planted the first 1,200 trees. The six varieties of apple trees included Braeburn, Fugi, Gala, Red Delicious, Yellow and Imagold. Different maturity dates provided a continuous supply of apples from late summer through late fall. None of the trees grew taller than seven feet to allow some ease of harvesting.

 During the first summer prior to the installation of the drip irrigation system the temperature exceeded 100 degrees and it was necessary for Dr. Elle and Howard Mercer to hand water the trees. Their efforts made it possible to save all of the trees during this hot summer. The following year 1,300 additional trees were planted and all blossoms removed from the first trees planted.

Armed with slides taken during the planting of the orchard PDG Breaud (Lubbock) and Howard Mercer (Southwest Lubbock) traveled the district acquainting Rotarians with the apple orchard project and seeking support from all Clubs. Through contributions by Rotarians the District Apple Orchard Committee was able to purchase much needed supplies and some equipment for the orchard. A special spraying unit was obtained, a fence was erected around the orchard and plans were made to build a pole barn on the property. The first harvest of apples was scheduled for the middle of August, 1997.

The South Plains Food Bank planned to distribute these apples at Thanksgiving and Christmas in the food boxes with the help of its 255 member agencies throughout the South Plains and through its affiliation with the High Plains Food Bank in Amarillo and the Permian Basin Food Bank in Odessa. The three food banks were affiliated through the Second Harvest Network of Food Banks. Within ten years the orchard was expected to produce 300,000 pounds of apples annually.

Rotarians contributed over $28,000 to this project which was far from finished in 1997. (Please refer to Chapter X for additional information.) Future plans included the development of on-site facilities for processing the crop.

APPENDIX XIII

 District 5730 Leadership Plan
Adopted May 3, 1997

BE IT RESOLVED that the District Governor of Rotary International District 5730, in counsel with the District Advisory Committee, Consisting of the Past District Governors, the District’s past director of Rotary International, and the District Governor Nominee, has decided that the leadership of the District for the year 1997-1998 and thereafter until otherwise resolved by the then current District Governor shall be as follows:

The District Leadership Plan has been developed with a focus on four primary goals:
 1) To lighten the work of the District Governor to permit younger Rotarians to serve in the office.
2)To permit the district to add new clubs without dividing the district.
3) To utilize more effectively the talent and commitment of past club presidents as district leaders.
4) To provide better assistance to clubs and their officers.

Although the objectives of less work, it is believed that all of the goals can be achieved by:
1) Increased delegation of duties.
2) More involvement of past club presidents as district leaders.
3) Improved selection and training of district leaders.

By appointing Assistant Governors to form a district leadership team, the District Governor delegates much of the administration work associated with club operations. The Governor then can devote more time to motivating Rotarians to participate in club and district activities and projects. The Governor can better emphasize the importance of strong membership by attending charter nights, including ceremonies, Foundation recognition programs, Foundation Seminars, and membership seminars. Furthermore, Governors are better able to personally recognize contributions of individual Rotarians, such as Paul Harris Fellows and recipients of Presidential Citations and District Awards. Formerly, such individual recognition was often impossible because Governors couldn’t spare time from administrative duties.

SELECTION OF THE DISTRICT GOVERNOR
The Nomination Committee will be composed of the three most recent past District Governors and now two current club presidents appointed by the current District Governor. The immediate past District Governor will serve as Chairman. This procedure is in place in this District but has not been formalized.

Substitution(s) to the Committee must be made if one of the DGND candidates is a member of the same club and/or resides in the same city as a committee member. Past District Governor substitutes will be made from the descending chronological list based on the years served as District Governor, the most recent Past District Governor serving as Chairman.

The District Governor will be selected for a one-year term as specified in the Rotary International bylaws. However, the District Governor will no longer be expected to bear the sole responsibility for recruiting and training a district team, often without a thorough knowledge of the most capable past club presidents available for service. Assistance will be provided by District Governors, from the time of their confirmation as District Governor Nominee, by a district organization and structure which has continuity and depth.



SELECTION OF ASSISTANT GOVERNORS

Like district committee chairs, Assistant Governors are district appointees selected by the incoming Governor. They are not officers of Rotary International. Assistant Governors are to be appointed on an annual basis, with no Assistant Governor serving more than three one-year terms. This provision allows continuity in the district leadership while also providing more leaders with an opportunity to serve. Past District Governors will not serve as an Assistant Governor.

 Essentially, the District Leadership Plan enables Districts to change the Governor’s emphasis from club visitation to being a leader in exploring and developing new means of meeting the District’s needs. This shift in focus also allows Rotary to project a more vital image by enabling younger leaders who are still active in their vocation to assume the role of Governor because of the more reasonable distribution of administrative duties.

Selection of the Assistant Governors with the approval of the District Governor will be by the District Nomination Committee per the following sequence of events:

1) June 15th – Official call inviting clubs to submit recommendations for consideration by the District Governor.
2) July 15th – Deadline for receipt of club recommendations of nominees.
3) September 1st – Deadline for receipt of nominees. The District Governor Nominee will promptly notify all candidates of the decision by telephone or mail.
4) October 1st – Deadline for notifying clubs of the candidates selected to be Assistant Governors.

 Minimum recommended criteria in selecting Assistant Governors include:
1) Membership, other then honorary, in good standing in a club in the district for at least three years.
2) Served as President of a club for a full term.
3) Willingness and ability to accept the responsibilities of Assistant Governor.
4) Demonstrated outstanding performance at the club and district level.
5) Potential as future Governor of the district.

GOVERNOR’S CABINET

Although the authority and responsibility for district administration remains vested in the District Governor, some authority, responsibility and accountability will be delegated to the members of the Governor’s Cabinet. Through such delegation the Governor will have more time to monitor the activities of the cabinet, district committees, and club officers. The members of the cabinet will be:

The District Governor
The District Governor Nominee
At the time of the confirmation, the District Governor Nominee Designates (to sit as an observer only)
The District Treasurer and District Secretary
The Assistant Governors

LEADERSHIP SELECTION & TRAINING

The improved identification and training of district leaders is critical to the success of the Proposed Plan. The following procedures will be utilized for the various levels:
District Governor Nominee. The District Governor Nominee will be invited to all meetings of the District cabinet.
Committee Chairpersons. The committee chairpersons for the next Rotary year will be selected by the cabinet. Normally, the chairpersons will be selected at least four months prior to taking office on July 1st. Their terms of office will be one year, but no one will serve in the same committee chairpersons for more than 3 consecutive terms. The committee chairperson will be trained in their duties and in relevant Rotary information at least six months prior to taking office.

 AMBASSADORIAL SCHOLARSHIPS

The application process for the outbound Rotary Foundation International Scholarships will be as follows: (1991 District Conference)
March 1st – Deadline for receipt of completed applications by sponsoring clubs.
April 1st – Deadline for receipt of club endorsed applications by the District Governor.
May 1st – Begin interview process.
June 30th – Final date for submission of District endorsed applications to the Rotary Foundation.

GROUP STUDY EXCHANGE

A Rotary International Foundation funded Group Study Exchange will be coordinated by the GSE Committee and approved by the District Governor's Cabinet. One exchange may be scheduled each year without specific approval of the clubs.

AMENDMENTS OF POLLICIES & PROCEDURES

Amendments to these policies and procedures may be recommended on an interim basis as necessary by the Governor’s Cabinet, and submitted for approval to clubs on month prior to the next District Conference, seeking approval at the next District Conference. Clubs may submit proposed amendments with approval of the majority of their members in writing, to the District Governor by February 1st each year, to be considered at the next District Conference. Such proposals will be provided to each club by March 1st.

CUSTOMS & TRADITIONS

The following customs and traditions are included here as a guide:

Governor’s Lapel Pin – It is the custom of the outgoing Governor to attend the final regular meeting of the incoming District Governor’s club during the Rotary year, or on the occasion of his/her installation, at which time the Governor’s lapel pin will be presented to the successor.

Past District Governor’s Lapel Pin – Annually, the immediate Past District Governor presents to the outgoing District Governor, at the occasion of the incoming District Governor’s installation or last club meeting of the Rotary year, a Past District Governor’s lapel pin with a diamond insert (not to exceed 20 points). The lapel pin is to be obtained by the immediate Past District Governor and paid for by District funds.

Gathering of the Council of Governors at the District Conference – The Council of Governors and the Past District Governor’s annual dinner should be open to all Past District Governors within the district still wanting to attend. All Past District Governors living in the district and still active in a Rotary club with the district should be invited.

Deaths of Club Presidents – Upon the death of a serving Club President the District Governor will send flowers and either the District Governor or his/her personal representative will attend the funeral.

Death of Past RI Officers – Upon the death of a Past RI Officer the District Governor will send flowers and either the District Governor or his/her personal representative will attend the funeral. The District Governor will notify RI immediately, as past officers of RI residing in the District.

The District Leadership Plan strengthens Rotary at both the district and club levels by making possible:

1) Faster and more responsive support to clubs
2) A larger supply of well-trained leaders in the district
3) A tested model which permits cost-effective growth
4) A larger field of candidates of governor
5) Improved participation in Foundation programs and district activities
6) Better communication at all levels in the district
7) A more challenging role for the governor

THE GOVERNOR’S OFFICIAL VISIT

This District Organization structure allows the Governor to visit clubs throughout the year individually or in multi, inter-club meetings for the purpose of :

1) Focusing attention on Important Rotary issues
2) Providing special attention to weak or struggling clubs
3) Motivating Rotarians to participate in service activities
4) Personally recognizing the outstanding contributes of Rotarians in the district

Such visits are to take place at a time that maximizes the impact of the governor’s presence, including charter nights, induction ceremonies, new-member orientation programs, Foundation events, or meetings. Multi-club or inter-city meeting should emphasize strong attendance from all participating clubs, not just the host club.

All Governors should still provide for district personal contact with each club through some form of official visit during the year. However, as outlined in the plan, governors will have more flexibility in scheduling their visits to clubs and can more effectively manage their time with respect to significant events through the year in each club.

THE DISTRICT TRAINER

The District Trainer’s role is an ongoing and a vital part of developing club and district leaders. The District Trainer will coordinate efforts with appropriate past district leadership in the training of Assistant Governors in addition to his/her training duties at the district assembly.

METHOD OF TRAINING ASSISTANT GOVERNORS

The governor-nominee is responsible for developing and implementing a training program for assistant governors with the support of the Council of Past Governors and the district trainer.

An effective program for training Assistant Governor address the following topics:

Joint developments of district goals
Duties and responsibilities
The relationship between the Governor and Assistant Governor
Club administration
 Conducting club assemblies
 The system for official visits
RI theme and Presidential Citation, if any
District finances
 Membership and extension
 Rotary information/RI resources
RI programs
Foundation programs
RI meetings (the Convention, Presidential Conferences, etc.)
Task forces and district committees

FUNDING THE EXPENSES OF ASSISTANT GOVERNORS

The Assistant Governors will be allowed a fee of $50.00 per month for expense money (this is a budget item that will be reviewed each year by the District Governor). This will cover postage, phone and travel expenses. This expense money will be paid in arrears beginning July 1st of the year you served as the Assistant Governor.

 DISTRICT CONFERNECE

A conference of Rotarians will be held annually within the District, during March, April, or May. The Governor’s Cabinet with an adjoining District. A majority of the District Clubs must approve holding a District Conference outside of District 5730 and approved by RI.

REFERENCES - Rotary International Manual of Procedure

DISTRICT BUDGET

The Governor’s Cabinet, with the assistance of the District Finance Committee, will prepare a recommended budget for the following Rotary year and will present it to all District Rotary Clubs at least four weeks in advance of the District Assembly, where the budget and District dues will be acted on by incoming Presidents.

MANAGEMENT OF FUNDS

Funds available per the District Budget shall be managed in accordance with the fiscal policy as set forth in the RI Manual of Procedure. Any expenditures from the Reserve Funds, if not included in the District Budget must be approved by the Governor’s Cabinet.
